[ΕΚΤΟΣ]               and Hort, and the Revisers of 1881

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]PREFACE
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The hidden depths both of the wisdom and knowledge of God were
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]manifest, not only in the revelation of his will contained in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Scriptures of truth, but in the manner of giving that revelation, and in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the language in which is was given.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Egypt had wisdom, but it was enshrined in hieroglyphics so obscure that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]their meaning faded centuries ago from the memory of mankind, and for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]many successive ages no man on earth could penetrate their mysteries.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Assyria and Babylon had literature, art, and science; but with a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]language written in seven or eight hundred cuneiform signs, some of them
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]having fifty different meanings, what wonder is it that for more than
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]two thousand years the language and literature of these nations was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lost, buried, and forgotten? The vast literature of China has survived
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the changes of centuries, but the list of different characters, which in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a dictionary of the second century numbered 9353, and in the latest
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]imperial Chinese Dictionary numbers 43,960,—some of them requiring
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]fifty strokes of the pencil to produce them,—shows how unfit such a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]language must be for a channel to convey the glad tidings of God's
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]salvation to the poor, the weak, the sorrowful, and to people who cannot
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]spend ten or twenty years in learning to comprehend the mysteries of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Chinese tongue.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Who can imagine what would have been the fate of a divine revelation if
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the words of eternal life had been enswathed in such cerements as these?
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In the wisdom of God, the revelation of his will was given in the Hebrew
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tongue, with an alphabet of twenty-two letters, some of which, as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inscribed on the Moabite stone, b.c. 900, are identical in form and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sound with those now used in English books.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This Hebrew alphabet, so simple that a child might learn it in a day,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]has never been lost or forgotten. The Hebrew language in which the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Oracles of God were given to man, has never become a dead language.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Since the day when the Law was given to Moses on Mount Sinai, there
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]never has been a day or hour when the language in which it was written
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was not known to living men, who were able to read, write, and expound
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]it. And the Hebrew is the only language of those ages that has lived to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the present time, preserving the record of a divine revelation, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]being conserved by it through the vicissitudes of conflict, conquest,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]captivity, and dispersion; while the surrounding idolatrous nations
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]perished in their own corruption, and their languages and literature
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]were buried in oblivion.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In later ages, when the gospel of the Son of God was to be proclaimed to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]all mankind, another language was used as a vehicle for its
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]communication. The bulk of the Israelitish race, through their
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]captivities and eternal associations, had lost the knowledge of the holy
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tongue, and had learned the languages of the Gentiles among whom they
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]dwelt; and now as their corporate national existence was to be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]interrupted, and they were to be dispersed among the peoples of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]earth, the Hebrew language was not a fit channel for conveying this
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]revelation to the Gentile world. Hence the same wise Providence which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]chose the undying Hebrew tongue for the utterances of the prophets,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]selected the Greek, which was at that time, more nearly than any other,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]a universal language, as the medium through which the teachings of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Saviour and the messages of the apostles should be sent forth to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]mankind.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This language, like the Hebrew, has maintained its existence,—though
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]it has been somewhat changed by the flight of years,—and the modern
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Greek spoken in Athens to-day is substantially the Greek of 1800 years
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ago.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The gospel of Christ was to go forth to every nation; and the miracle of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Pentecost indicated that it was the Divine purpose that each nation
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]should hear in their own tongue wherein they were born, the wonderful
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]works of God. Hence the Scriptures have been translated into hundreds of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]languages, and to-day six hundred millions of people, comprising all the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]leading races and nations of the earth, may have access to the Word of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]God in their native tongues. Nevertheless, no translation can perfectly
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]express the delicate shades of thought which are uttered in another
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]language, and it often becomes necessary and desirable to recur to the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]original Scriptures, and by searching them to find out the precise
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]meaning of those words which were given by the Holy Ghost, and which are
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]"more to be desired than gold, yea, than much fine gold." For while,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]speaking in a general way, we have faithful translations, which give us
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]with great accuracy the sense of the Scriptures as a whole, yet there
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]are times when we desire fuller and more accurate information concerning
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]particular words uttered by those men to whom the Holy Ghost was given
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to bring all things to their remembrance, to guide them into all truth,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and to show them things to come. Frequently there are depths of meaning
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which the casual reader does not fathom, and the study of the Greek and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Hebrew becomes as needful as it is agreeable to those who love God's
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]law, who delight in his gospel, and who have time and opportunity to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]prosecute such studies.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]There are few lovers of the Bible who do not at times wish that they
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]might clearly know the precise sense of some one original word which may
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sometimes be obscurely translated; or who would not be delighted to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inquire of some competent scholar as to the meaning of certain
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]expressions contained in that Book of God. Such persons are glad to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]study the original Scriptures, that they may learn, as far as possible,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]exactly what God has said to man.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The learning of a living language from those who seek it is no trifling
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]task; but a language which must be learned from books, presents much
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]greater difficulties; and to many persons the mastery of the Greek
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]tongue looks like the labor of a lifetime. It is; and yet it is possible
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for studious Bible readers to learn the things they specially need to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]know, with an amount of labor which need not appall or dishearten any
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]diligent student.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]There are contained in the Greek New Testament about 5594 words; but in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the whole Greek literature the words are a great multitude, which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]perhaps no man has ever numbered. The student of the Scriptures is not
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]anxious to master or explore this vast wealth of Greek literature, but
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his ambition is to know something of those 5594 New Testament words in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which the message of God's mercy is conveyed to fallen man. Hence he may
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pass by the bulk of Greek literature, and confine his investigations to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]those few Greek words which are used in the New Testament to convey to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]man the treasures of Divine truth.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]A lexicon of the entire Greek language might extend through thousands of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pages. There stands on the shelves of the writer a copy of Stephen's
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]"Thesaures Grææ Linguæ," in eight folio volumes, (London, 1816-1825,)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which contains more than thirteen thousand large, closely printed pages.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]A later edition of the same work is still larger; and there are critics
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and students who would find use for a lexicon as elaborate as this; but
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]for the great mass of Biblical students most of this material would be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]entirely useless, and a very small volume would contain all they would
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]find necessary in their study of the Scriptures of truth. Thus a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]portable volume, confining itself to the definition of the words
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]included in the New Testament, could give them fuller treatment than a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]very large volume which, covering the entire language would be obliged
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to condense and abridge at every point, omitting perhaps the very matter
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]most desired by a student of the Sacred Word.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]There is another consideration; the Greek of the New Testament differs
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]materially in its character from the classical Green, or from the Greek
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]language as a whole. About b.c. 288, the law of Moses was translated
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]from Hebrew into Greek, by request of Ptolemy Philadelphus, who sought
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]everywhere for books to enrich his great library at Alexandria.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Subsequently other portions were translated, and hence, we have what is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]known as the Septuagint version of the Old Testament. This was largely
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]used among the Jewish people in Egypt and elsewhere in the time of our
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Saviour. It was a Greek version of a Hebrew book, and it was through
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this book, as well as by intercourse with Grecian people and foreigners
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Jewish stock, that the Jewish people became acquainted with the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Greek tongue. But the Greek of the Septuagint was full of Hebrew ideas
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and idioms, and hence the Greek of the Jews in Palestine, and of the New
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament which is also saturated with Old Testament ideas, differed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]from the ordinary language of the Greeks, new meanings having been
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]imparted to various words, in order that they might represent Hebrew
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]words and Hebrew ideas. For example, when the Greek word hades is used,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]we are not thereby committed to a belief in all the fabulous ideas of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Greeks concerning the abode of the dead, for the word was but the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]representative of the Hebrew word sheol, which is almost always
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]translated hades in the Greek Testament. Hence in studying this Greek
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]word in the New Testament we are not to go to the Greek classics to find
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]out what they mean by the word hades, but rather to the Hebrew prophets
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to learn in what sense they use the Hebrew word sheol, of which it is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Greek equivalent. So the word diatheke in classic Greek signifies a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]will, testament, covenant, and so the term in the common English version
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]is translated in both these ways. But in the Septuagint the word
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]diatheke is used to translate the Hebrew word berith, which signifies a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]covenant, but does not signify a will or testament.1* Hence, instead of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]speaking of "The New Testament" we should more correctly call it the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]"The New Covenant." A variety of instances might be adduced where New
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament Greek words have meaning widely different from the same words
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]in classic Greek.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]It must also be remembered, that there are words in the New Testament
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]which are not found at all in the classical writers. When new ideas are
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to be conveyed, new words must be found to convey them. In the language
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Hindus there is no word for home, simply because the Hindu has no
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]home. The idea of a home as understood by Christians, is utterly foreign
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the Hindu nation and religion. There are heathen nations that have no
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]word for gratitude, because gratitude is unknown to them; so the word
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]agape or charity, which describes unselfish love, a love which reaches
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to enemies, and which seeks no personal gratification or reward,—that
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]love which is of God, and concerning which it is said, "God is Love,"
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]refers to something unknown to the heathen world. They had no word to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]express it, because they had not the thing itself to express.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]These illustrations indicate some of the peculiarities of the New
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament Greek, and serve to show use why the student of the Bible
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]needs an especial apparatus for studying the Greek New Testament. For
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]this purpose, he has been most generously furnished with New Testament
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lexicons, in different languages, Latin, German, and English, by the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]labor of devout and learned students. Among the most noteworthy may be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]mentioned Robinson's Greek Lexicon of the New Testament, and Prof. J. H.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Thayer's later and more elaborate Greek-English Lexicon of the New
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament, based upon Prof. Grimm's get Lexicon; which is well adapted
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to meet the needs of the critical student.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]The meaning of words is determined finally by their usage by those who
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]employ them, and the only way to settle the sense of disputed and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]difficult words is carefully to examine each word in its connection, in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]all the passages where it is used, and in the light of that examination
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]decide as to its meaning or meanings. In some of the more elaborate
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]lexicons a large number of passages are thus cited, illustrating the use
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the words under consideration, and it some cases authors have
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]endeavored to give nearly every passage where a word occurs in the New
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament. This however, cumbers the lexicon with a large amount of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]material which in most instances is of little use. This examination and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]comparison of passages, the careful student should make for himself, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his interests in this direction are better served by the use of a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]concordance; and for those who wish to investigate carefully the meaning
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Greek words in the New Testament, ample provision has been made in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the Critical Greek and English Concordance, prepared by Prof. C. F.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Hudson, under the direction of H. L. Hastings, and revised and completed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by the late Ezra Abbot, D.D., LL.D., Profession of New Testament
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Criticism and Interpretation in the Divinity School of Harvard
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]University.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This book contains (1) the more than five thousand words in the Greek
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament, in alphabetical order. (2) Reference to every passage where
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]those words occur. (3) Every English word which is used to translate the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]given Greek word. (4) The passages in which the Greek word is translated
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]by each English word, classified and set by themselves, so that the more
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]usual translations are also exhibited. (5) The various readings of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]leading critical editions and manuscripts of the Greek Testament. (6) An
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]English index, so that with this book a person who does not know a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]letter of the Greek alphabet is enabled to find the original for any
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]English word in the New Testament, learn in how many places it occurs,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and in how many ways it is translated, so that by examining every
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]passage, he may have the data before him for making up and independent
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]judgment from the facts in the case.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This Greek Concordance obviates the necessity for an exhaustive citation
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the passages containing any particular Greek word, and brings us back
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]to the proper sphere of a Lexicon, as a book defining the words
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]contained in the language, with occasional references to passages which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]illustrate the different meanings; and when equipped with Hudson's
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Concordance, a manual lexicon of New Testament Greek serves the purpose
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the ordinary student.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]It is for the accommodation of such students, who know little of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Greek language, but who desire to "search the Scriptures," and of others
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]more scholarly, who yet find it needful to refresh their memories as to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]the sense of Greek words, that this Lexicon is offered. It was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]originally prepared by William Greenfield, who was born in London, in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]1799, and died there November 5, 1831. He edited Bagster's Comprehensive
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Bible, a Syriac New Testament, a Hebrew New Testament, and prepared an
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]abridgement of Schmidt's Greek Concordance. In 1830 he was appointed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]editor of the Foreign Versions of the British and Foreign Bible Society;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and though he died at an early age, he had a high reputation as an
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]accomplished linguist.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]In preparing his Lexicon of the New Testament, he drew materials from
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]every accessible source; the Lexicons of Parkhurst, Schleusner, Wahl,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]and Robinson being especially examined and laid under contribution. This
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Lexicon, issued by Bagster & Sons as a companion for a portable edition
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of the Greek Testament, served an excellent purpose. At a later date it
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]was carefully revised with numerous additions and improvements, by Rev.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Thomas Sheldon Green, M.A. and it has received the hearty approval of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]competent Greek scholars, like the late Professor Ezra Abbot of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Cambridge.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Though the body of this Lexicon includes all the words contained in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Received Text of the Greek New Testament, yet in the texts now more or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]less current, in particular those of Lachmann, Tischendorf, Tregelles,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Westcott and Hort, and the Westminster Revised, certain new words and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]forms are introduced, not found in the Received Text.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]With a desire to give completeness to this Lexicon, a list of these
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]words is presented, with definitions and a record of the places where
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]they occur, at the end of the Lexicon. This list has been prepared by
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Mr. Wallace N. Stearns, under the supervision of Prof. J. Henry Thayer,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of Harvard Divinity School, the successor of the lamented Dr. Ezra
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Abbot, and one of the revisers of the New Testament, whose arduous
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]labors in the department of sacred lexicography are too well-known to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]need further mention.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]With these statements as to the object and character of this Lexicon, we
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]commit this new edition to the kind of providence of Him whose words of
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]truth are therein expounded, and without whose blessings all labor and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]effort is but in vain.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]H.L. Hastings
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Scriptural Tract Repository,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Boston, Mass., June, 1896.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]EXPLANATIONS
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Allusion has been made in the preface to certain peculiarities of New
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament Greek, which distinguish it from the classic Greek of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]heathen world.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]This Lexicon indicates some of these peculiarities, by distinguishing
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]three classes of words:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]I. Later Greek words, marked L. G., the occurrence of which may be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]regarded as commencing within the Later Greek period, which is here
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]reckoned from and includes the writing of the historian Polybius, B.C.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]204-123.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]II. New Testament words, marked N. T., which only occur in the New
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament, or if found elsewhere are only in certain peculiar quarters.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]III. Septuagint words, marked S., which besides their occurrence in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]New Testament are found only in the Septuagint Version of the Old
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Testament, the Greek Apocryphal books, and kindred writings; and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]meaning of which is to be studied, not in the usage of classical Greek
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]writings, but rather in the light of the Hebrew Old Testament, and the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]writings of Jewish authors who were familiar with Hebrew ideas and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Hebrew literature.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ABBREVIATIONS, ETC.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]comp. 	comparative.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]conj. 	conjunction.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]contr. 	contraction, or contracted.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]dat. 	dative case.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]dimin. 	diminutive.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]enclit. 	enclitic, throwing the accent on the preceding syllable.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]& et. 	and.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]e.g. 	exempli gratia, for example.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]gen. 	genitive case.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]genr. 	generally, in a general sense, not affected by adjuncts.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Heb. 	Hebrew, or the Hebrew idiom.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]i.e. 	id est, that is.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]idem. 	the same.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]i.q. 	same as.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]inf. 	infinitive mood.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]int. interj. 	interjection.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]meton. 	by metonymy
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]metath. 	metathesis, the transportation of letter.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]mid. 	middle voice.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]N.T. 	New Testament
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]opt. 	optative mood.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]O.T. 	Old Testament
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]part. 	participle.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pass. 	passive voice.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]r. per. 	perfect tense.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]plu. p. 	pluperfect tense.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pl. 	plural.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]pr. 	properly.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]preced. 	preceding.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]quater 	four times.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sc. 	scilicet, that is to say, that is.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]seq. 	sequente, as, seq. gen. sequente genitivo, with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]		a genitive following.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sing. 	signular–the figure, placed before sign. or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]		pl. denote the person.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]spe. 	specially, i.e. in a special and local meaning.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subj. 	subjunctive mood.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subs. 	substantive.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]superl. 	superlative.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ter. 	thrice.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]trans. 	transitively.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]trop. 	tropically, i.e. turned aside from its strick
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]		literal meaning.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]v. 	vel, or.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]v.r. 	a various reading to the commond text.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]vix. 	videlicet, that is, namely
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]voc. 	vocative case
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ὀ attached to a word show it to be masculine;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ἡ, to be feminine;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]ὁ ἡ to be common, i.e. masculine or feminine; and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]τό, to be neuter.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Α, α, Ἄλφα

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Α, α, Alpha,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    the first letter in the Greek Alphabet, and used for the first,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	Re. 1.8, 11; 21.6; 22.13.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    In composition, it denotes privation; sometimes augmentation,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	and union.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Αβᾰρής, έος, οῦς, ὁ, ἡ, τὸ, -ές, (ἀ & βάρος)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    not burdensome, not chargeable, 2 Co. 11.9.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    ind. ch. or Sy. אבא, father, Mar. 14.36. Ro.8.15 Ga. 4.6.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    pr. bottomless; place of the dead, hell.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	significations, must be learnt from the grammars.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	Standing at the commencement of a clause, it is another
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	form of ἐάν, if Jno. 20.23.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    a son, Mat. 16.17.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    a barbarian, pr. one to whom a pure Greek dialect is

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	Col 3.11. Ac. 28.2, 4; a foreigner speaking a strange
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	language, 1 Co. 14.11.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Βαρέω, ῶ, (βάρος)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    f. ήσω, p. pass. βεβάρημαι,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    to be heavy upon, weigh down, burden, oppress, as sleep,

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    adv. heavily; met. with difficulty, dully, stupidly,

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Βασανίζω, (βάσανος)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    f. ίσω, a.1. pass. ἐβασανίσθην,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    pr. to apply the lapis Lydius or touchstone;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	met. to examine, scrutinise, try either by words
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	or torture; in N.T. to afflict, torment;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	pass. to be afflicted, tormented, pained, by diseases,

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    pr. examination by the lapis Lydius or by torture;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	torment, torture, Re. 9.5; 14.11; 18.7, 10, 15.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Βασανιστής, οῦ, ὁ,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    pr. an inquisitor, tormentor; in N.T. a keeper of a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	prison, gaoler, Mat. 18.34.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Βάσᾰνος, ου, ἡ,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    pr. lapis Lydius, a species of stone from Lydia,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	which being applied to metals was thought to indicate
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	any alloy which might be mixed with them, and therefore
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	used in the trial of metals; hence, examination of a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	person, especially by torture; in N.T. torture, torment,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	severe pain, Mat. 4.24. Lu. 16.23, 28.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Βασιλεία, ας, ἡ (βασιλεύς)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    a kingdom, realm, the region or country governed
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	by a king; kingly power, authority, dominion, reign;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	royal dignity, the title and honour of king;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	ἡ βασιλεία, Mat. 9.35, ἡ βασιλεία τοῦ Θεοῦ,—τοῦ
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	Χριστοῦ,—τοῦ οὐρανοῦ,—τῶν οὐρανῶν, the reign or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	kingdom of the Messiah, both in a false and true
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	conception of it; used also, with various limitation,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	of its administration and coming history as in the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	parables; its distinctive nature, Ro. 14.17, requirements,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	privileges, rewards, consummation.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Βασίλειος, ου, ὁ, ἡ,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    royal, regal; met. possessed of high prerogatives and

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	mansions, palaces, Lu. 7.25:

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Βασιλεύς, έως, ὁ,

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    a causal particle or conjunction, for; it is, however, frequently

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    an enclitic particle imparting emphasis; indicating that a particular
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	regard is to be had to the term to which it is attached. Its force is
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	to be conveyed, when this is possible, by various expression: at least,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	indeed, even, &c.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	for the horrid worship of Moloch, & afterwards polluted with every
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	species of filth, as well as the carcases of animals, and dead bodies
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	of malefactors; to consume which, in order to avert the pestilence

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	kept burning; hence, hell, the fires of Tartarus, the place of

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δέ,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    a conjunctive particle, marking the superaddition of a clause,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	whether in opposition or in continuation, to what has preceded,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	and it may be variously rendered but, on the other hand, and,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	also, now, &c; καὶ δέ, when there is a special superaddition in
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	continuation, too, yea, &c. It sometimes is found at the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	commencement of the apodosis of a sentence, Ac. 11.17. It serves
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	also to mark the resumption of an interrupted discourse,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	2 Co. 2.10. Gal. 2.6
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δέησις, εως, ἡ, (δέομαι)

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	et al.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    it is binding, it is necessary, it behoveth, it is proper;

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    to lack, fall short of;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    mid. δέομαι, f. δεήσομαι, a.1. ἐδεήθην,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    to be in want, to need; to ask, request, Mat. 9.38.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	8.22, 24, et al.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δή,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    a particle serving to add an intensity of expression
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	to a term or clause. Its simplest and most ordinary uses
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	are when it gives impressiveness to an affirmation,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	indeed, really, doubtless, Mat. 13.23. 2 Co. 12.1,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	or earnestness to a call, injunction or entreaty,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	Lu. 2.15. Ac. 13.2; 15.36. 1 Co. 6.20.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δῆλος, η, ον,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    pr. clearly visible; plain, manifest, evident,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	Mat. 26.73. 1 Co. 15.27. Gal. 3.11. 1 Ti. 6.7:

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δηλόω, ῶ
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    f. ώσω, a.1. ἐδήλωσα,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    to render manifest or evident; to make known, to tell,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	relate, declare, 1 Co. 1.11. Col. 1.8; to show, point out,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	bring to light, 1 Co. 3.13, to intimate, signify,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	He. 9.8; 12.27; 1 Pe. 1.11.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    f. ήσω,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    to address a public assembly, to deliver an harangue or
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	public oration, Ac. 12.21.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δημιουργός, οῦ, ὁ, (δῆμος, & ἔργον)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    pr. one who labours for the public, or, exercises some

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    the people, Ac. 12.22; 17.5; 19.30, 33:

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δημόσιος, ία, ον,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    public, belonging to the public, Ac. 5.18; δημοσίᾳ,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	publicly, Ac. 16.37; 18.28; 20.20.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δηνάριον, ίου, τό,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    Lat. denarius, a Roman silver coin. The silver denarius
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	was at first equivalent to about 8 ½d. of English money,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	declining, under the empire, to about 7 ½d., and was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	therefore somewhat less than the Greek δραχμή. The name
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	originally imported ten ases.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δήποτε,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    an intensive combination of the particle δή with πότε;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	which see; as an intensive, Jno. 5.4.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Δήπου,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    see που.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Διά,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    prep., with a genitive, through, used of place or medium,

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    f. ζήσω & ζήσομαι, a.1. ἔζησα, p. ἔζηκα,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    to live, to be possessed of vitality, to exercise the functions
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	of life, Mat. 27.63. Ac. 17.28, et al.; τὸ ζῆν, life, He. 2.15;
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	to have means of subsistence, 1 Co. 9.14; to live, to pass existence
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	in a specific manner, Lu. 2.36; 15.13, et al.; to be instinct with
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	life and vigour; hence, ζῶν, living, an epithet of God, in a sense
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	peculiar to Himself; ἐλπὶς ζῶσα, a vigorous and enduring hope,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	1 Pe. 1.3; ὕδωρ ζῶν, a perennial flow of water, Jno 4.10; to be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	cheered and happy, 1 Thes. 3.8; to be exempt from spiritual
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	condemnation, to have fruition of salvation, 1 Jno. 4.9, et.al.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	ὀψὲ σαββάτων, after the close of the sabbath, Mat. 28.1:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	whence
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Ὄψῐμος, ου, ὁ, ἡ,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    late; latter, Ja. 5.7; poetic and later prose for
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Ὄψιος, ία, ιον,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    late, Mar. 11.11; ἡ ὀψία, sc. ὥρα, evening, two of which
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	were reckoned by the Hebrews; one from the ninth hour,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	until sunset, Mat. 8.16; 14.15, et al.; and the other,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	from sunset until dark, Mat. 14.23; 16.2, et al.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Παιδαγωγός, οῦ, ἡ, (παῖς & ἀγωγός, ἄγω)
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]    a pedagogue, child-tender, a person, usually a slave or freedman,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	to whom the boys of a family were committed, whose duty it was
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	to attend them at their play, lead them to and from the public
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	school, and exercise a constant superintendence over their conduct
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	and safety; in N.T., an ordinary director or minister contrasted
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	with an Apostle, as a pedagogue occupies an inferior position
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	to a parent, 1 Co. 4.15; a term applied to the Mosaic Law as
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	dealing with men as in a state of mere childhood and tutelage,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	Ga. 3.24, 25.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	which as a weight was among the Jews equivalent

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	114lbs. 15dwts. Troy, while the Attic talent,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	on the usual estimate, was only equal to
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	56lbs. 11oz. troy; and as a denomination of money,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	it was equal among the former to 342£. 3s. 9d.,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	or if reckoned of gold, 5,475£., and among the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	latter to 198£. 15s., or 225£., or 243£. 15s.
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	sterling, according to various estimates,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]	Mat. 18.24; 25.15, 16, 20, 24, 25, 28.

[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Foot notes:
[ΕΚΤΟΣ] 1*Both the Authorized and the Revised Versions of Heb. ix. 15-16 give
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]countenance to the assumption that our Saviour made his last will and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]testament, and by his death gave it validity. But in that case would not
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]his resurrection three days later, have rendered it null and void? The
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]subject under discussion in Hebrews ix. is not two testaments or wills,
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]but two covenants or agreements; and the death referred to seems to be
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]not the death of a "testator," or mediator but the death of the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sacrifice which was offered after the custom of ancient times when a
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]solemn covenant was made, and whose blood sprinkled on the ground and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]over the covenanting parties, sealed the covenant, which was of no force
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]until the sacrifice was slain. The first covenant was sealed with the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]blood of calves and goats; the second covenant with the blood of the Son
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]of God. Heb. ix. 15-26; xiii. 20; Exod. xxiv. 4-8; Gen. xv. 8-18; Mark
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]xiv. 24. He was not only the mediator of the new covenant, but also the
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]sacrifice which sealed it, all types combining in Him. For an extended
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]discussion of this subject, consulting Macknight on The Epistles, and
[ΕΚΤΟΣ]Granville Penn's Notes on The New Covenant. Heb. ix. 15-16.
